<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Electric motor</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Machine that converts electrical energy into mechanical energy</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other kinds of motors, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Motor_(disambiguation)" title="Motor (disambiguation)">Motor (disambiguation)</a>. For a railroad engine, see <a href="/wiki/Electric_locomotive" title="Electric locomotive">Electric locomotive</a>.</div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Electric_motor.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Electric_motor.gif/220px-Electric_motor.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Electric_motor.gif/330px-Electric_motor.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Electric_motor.gif/440px-Electric_motor.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Electric_motor.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation showing operation of a brushed DC electric motor</div></div></div>
<p>An <b>electric motor</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Electric_machine" title="Electric machine">electrical machine</a> that converts <a href="/wiki/Electrical_energy" title="Electrical energy">electrical energy</a> into <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_energy" title="Mechanical energy">mechanical energy</a>. Most electric motors operate through the interaction between the motor's <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric current</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_coil" title="Electromagnetic coil">wire winding</a> to generate force in the form of <a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">torque</a> applied on the motor's shaft. An <a href="/wiki/Electric_generator" title="Electric generator">electric generator</a> is mechanically identical to an electric motor, but operates with a reversed flow of power, converting mechanical energy into electrical energy.
</p><p>Electric motors can be powered by <a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">direct current</a> (DC) sources, such as from batteries, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rectifiers" title="Rectifiers">rectifiers</a>, or by <a href="/wiki/Alternating_current" title="Alternating current">alternating current</a> (AC) sources, such as a power grid, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inverter_(electrical)" title="Inverter (electrical)">inverters</a> or electrical generators.
</p><p>Electric motors may be classified by considerations such as power source type, construction, application and type of motion output. They can be powered by AC or DC, be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brushed_motor" title="Brushed motor">brushed</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brushless_motor" title="Brushless motor">brushless</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Single-phase" title="Single-phase">single-phase</a>, <a href="/wiki/Two-phase_electric_power" title="Two-phase electric power">two-phase</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three-phase" title="Three-phase">three-phase</a>, <a href="/wiki/Axial_flux_motor" title="Axial flux motor">axial</a> or radial flux, and may be air-cooled or liquid-cooled.
</p><p>Standardized motors provide convenient mechanical power for industrial use. The largest are used for ship propulsion, pipeline compression and <a href="/wiki/Pumped-storage_hydroelectricity" title="Pumped-storage hydroelectricity">pumped-storage</a> applications with output exceeding 100 <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">megawatts</a>.
</p><p>Applications include industrial fans, blowers and pumps, machine tools, household appliances, power tools, vehicles, and disk drives. Small motors may be found in electric watches. In certain applications, such as in <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_braking" title="Regenerative braking">regenerative braking</a> with <a href="/wiki/Traction_motor" title="Traction motor">traction motors</a>, electric motors can be used in reverse as generators to recover energy that might otherwise be lost as heat and friction.
</p><p>Electric motors produce linear or rotary force (<a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">torque</a>) intended to propel some external mechanism, such as a fan or an elevator. An electric motor is generally designed for continuous rotation, or for linear movement over a significant distance compared to its size. Magnetic <a href="/wiki/Solenoid" title="Solenoid">solenoids</a> are also transducers that convert electrical power to mechanical motion, but can produce motion over only a limited distance.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_electric_motor" title="History of the electric motor">History of the electric motor</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_motors">Early motors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early motors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faraday_magnetic_rotation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="545" data-file-width="538" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Faraday_magnetic_rotation.jpg/220px-Faraday_magnetic_rotation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Faraday_magnetic_rotation.jpg/330px-Faraday_magnetic_rotation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Faraday_magnetic_rotation.jpg/440px-Faraday_magnetic_rotation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Faraday_magnetic_rotation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Faraday's electromagnetic experiment, 1821, the first demonstration of the conversion of electrical energy into motion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faraday_(1822)2_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Faraday_(1822)2-1">[1]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Before modern electromagnetic motors, experimental motors that worked by electrostatic force were investigated. The first electric motors were simple <a href="/wiki/Electrostatic_motor" title="Electrostatic motor">electrostatic devices</a> described in experiments by Scottish monk <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Gordon_(Benedictine)" title="Andrew Gordon (Benedictine)">Andrew Gordon</a> and American experimenter <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a> in the 1740s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon2_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon2-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The theoretical principle behind them, <a href="/wiki/Coulomb%27s_law" title="Coulomb's law">Coulomb's law</a>, was discovered but not published, by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cavendish" title="Henry Cavendish">Henry Cavendish</a> in 1771. This law was discovered independently by <a href="/wiki/Charles-Augustin_de_Coulomb" title="Charles-Augustin de Coulomb">Charles-Augustin de Coulomb</a> in 1785, who published it so that it is now known with his name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Due to the difficulty of generating the high voltages they required, electrostatic motors were never used for practical purposes.
</p><p>The invention of the electrochemical battery by <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Volta" title="Alessandro Volta">Alessandro Volta</a> in 1799<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> made possible the production of persistent electric currents. <a href="/wiki/Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted" title="Hans Christian Ørsted">Hans Christian Ørsted</a> discovered in 1820 that an electric current creates a magnetic field, which can exert a force on a magnet. It only took a few weeks for <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9-Marie_Amp%C3%A8re" title="André-Marie Ampère">André-Marie Ampère</a> to develop the first formulation of the electromagnetic interaction and present the <a href="/wiki/Amp%C3%A8re%27s_force_law" title="Ampère's force law">Ampère's force law</a>, that described the production of mechanical force by the interaction of an electric current and a magnetic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dcmachine2_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-dcmachine2-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first demonstration of the effect with a rotary motion was given by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">Michael Faraday</a> on 3 September 1821 in the basement of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institution" title="Royal Institution">Royal Institution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Electric_Motion_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Electric_Motion-7">[7]</a></sup> A free-hanging wire was dipped into a pool of mercury, on which a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permanent_magnet" title="Permanent magnet">permanent magnet (PM)</a> was placed. When a current was passed through the wire, the wire rotated around the magnet, showing that the current gave rise to a close circular magnetic field around the wire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SparkMuseum_(Motor)2_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-SparkMuseum_(Motor)2-8">[8]</a></sup> Faraday published the results of his discovery in the <i><a href="/wiki/Quarterly_Journal_of_Science" title="Quarterly Journal of Science">Quarterly Journal of Science</a></i>, and sent copies of his paper along with pocket-sized models of his device to colleagues around the world so they could also witness the phenomenon of electromagnetic rotations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Electric_Motion_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Electric_Motion-7">[7]</a></sup> This motor is often demonstrated in physics experiments, substituting <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a> for (toxic) mercury. <a href="/wiki/Barlow%27s_wheel" title="Barlow's wheel">Barlow's wheel</a> was an early refinement to this Faraday demonstration, although these and similar <a href="/wiki/Homopolar_motor" title="Homopolar motor">homopolar motors</a> remained unsuited to practical application until late in the century.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jedlik_motor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="403" data-file-width="614" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Jedlik_motor.jpg/220px-Jedlik_motor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Jedlik_motor.jpg/330px-Jedlik_motor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Jedlik_motor.jpg/440px-Jedlik_motor.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jedlik_motor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/%C3%81nyos_Jedlik" title="Ányos Jedlik">Jedlik</a>'s "electromagnetic self-rotor", 1827 (Museum of Applied Arts, Budapest). The historic motor still works perfectly today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TravelHungary_(Dynamo)2_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-TravelHungary_(Dynamo)2-9">[9]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:An_electric_motor_presented_to_Kelvin_by_James_Joule_in_1842,_Hunterian_Museum,_Glasgow.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2056" data-file-width="3088" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/An_electric_motor_presented_to_Kelvin_by_James_Joule_in_1842%2C_Hunterian_Museum%2C_Glasgow.jpg/220px-An_electric_motor_presented_to_Kelvin_by_James_Joule_in_1842%2C_Hunterian_Museum%2C_Glasgow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/An_electric_motor_presented_to_Kelvin_by_James_Joule_in_1842%2C_Hunterian_Museum%2C_Glasgow.jpg/330px-An_electric_motor_presented_to_Kelvin_by_James_Joule_in_1842%2C_Hunterian_Museum%2C_Glasgow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/An_electric_motor_presented_to_Kelvin_by_James_Joule_in_1842%2C_Hunterian_Museum%2C_Glasgow.jpg/440px-An_electric_motor_presented_to_Kelvin_by_James_Joule_in_1842%2C_Hunterian_Museum%2C_Glasgow.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:An_electric_motor_presented_to_Kelvin_by_James_Joule_in_1842,_Hunterian_Museum,_Glasgow.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An electric motor presented to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_Kelvin" title="Lord Kelvin">Kelvin</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Joule" title="James Joule">James Joule</a> in 1842, Hunterian Museum, Glasgow</div></div></div>
<p>In 1827, <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungarian</a> <a href="/wiki/Physicist" title="Physicist">physicist</a> <a href="/wiki/%C3%81nyos_Jedlik" title="Ányos Jedlik">Ányos Jedlik</a> started experimenting with <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_coil" title="Electromagnetic coil">electromagnetic coils</a>. After Jedlik solved the technical problems of continuous rotation with the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Commutator_(electric)" title="Commutator (electric)">commutator</a>, he called his early devices "electromagnetic self-rotors". Although they were used only for teaching, in 1828 Jedlik demonstrated the first device to contain the three main components of practical <a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">DC</a> motors: the <a href="/wiki/Stator" title="Stator">stator</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rotor_(electric)" title="Rotor (electric)">rotor</a> and commutator. The device employed no permanent magnets, as the magnetic fields of both the stationary and revolving components were produced solely by the currents flowing through their windings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guillemin_(1891)2_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guillemin_(1891)2-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature2_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nature2-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blundel_(2012)2_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blundel_(2012)2-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thein_(2009)2_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thein_(2009)2-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Electropaedia_(home)2_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Electropaedia_(home)2-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="DC_motors">DC motors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: DC motors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/DC_motor" title="DC motor">DC motor</a></div>
<p>The first <a href="/wiki/Commutator_(electric)" title="Commutator (electric)">commutator</a> <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1023754711">.mw-parser-output .vanchor>:target~.vanchor-text{background-color:#b1d2ff}</style><span class="vanchor"><span id="DC_electric_motor"></span><span class="vanchor-text">DC electric motor</span></span> capable of turning machinery was invented by British scientist <a href="/wiki/William_Sturgeon" title="William Sturgeon">William Sturgeon</a> in 1832.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Following Sturgeon's work, a commutator-type direct-current electric motor was built by American inventor <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Davenport_(inventor)" title="Thomas Davenport (inventor)">Thomas Davenport</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emily_Davenport" title="Emily Davenport">Emily Davenport</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> which he patented in 1837. The motors ran at up to 600 revolutions per minute, and powered machine tools and a printing press.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garrison_(1998)2_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Garrison_(1998)2-19">[19]</a></sup> Due to the high cost of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">primary battery power</a>, the motors were commercially unsuccessful and bankrupted Davenport. Several inventors followed Sturgeon in the development of DC motors, but all encountered the same battery cost issues. As no <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_distribution" title="Electric power distribution">electricity distribution</a> system was available at the time, no practical commercial market emerged for these motors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nye_(1990)2_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nye_(1990)2-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>After many other more or less successful attempts with relatively weak rotating and reciprocating apparatus Prussian/Russian <a href="/wiki/Moritz_von_Jacobi" title="Moritz von Jacobi">Moritz von Jacobi</a> created the first real rotating electric motor in May 1834. It developed remarkable mechanical output power. His motor set a world record, which Jacobi improved four years later in September 1838.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> His second motor was powerful enough to drive a boat with 14 people across a wide river. It was also in 1839/40 that other developers managed to build motors with similar and then higher performance.
</p><p>In 1855, Jedlik built a device using similar principles to those used in his electromagnetic self-rotors that was capable of useful work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guillemin_(1891)2_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guillemin_(1891)2-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Electropaedia_(home)2_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Electropaedia_(home)2-16">[16]</a></sup> He built a model <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle" title="Electric vehicle">electric vehicle</a> that same year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hungarianscience_(Frankfurt)2_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hungarianscience_(Frankfurt)2-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>A major turning point came in 1864, when <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Pacinotti" title="Antonio Pacinotti">Antonio Pacinotti</a> first described the ring armature (although initially conceived in a DC generator, i.e. a dynamo).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dcmachine2_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-dcmachine2-6">[6]</a></sup> This featured symmetrically-grouped coils closed upon themselves and connected to the bars of a commutator, the brushes of which delivered practically non-fluctuating current.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Antonio_Pacinotti2_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Antonio_Pacinotti2-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klein2_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klein2-24">[24]</a></sup> The first commercially successful DC motors followed the developments by <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%A9nobe_Gramme" title="Zénobe Gramme">Zénobe Gramme</a> who, in 1871, reinvented Pacinotti's design and adopted some solutions by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Werner_Siemens" title="Werner Siemens">Werner Siemens</a>.
</p><p>A benefit to DC machines came from the discovery of the reversibility of the electric machine, which was announced by Siemens in 1867 and observed by Pacinotti in 1869.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dcmachine2_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-dcmachine2-6">[6]</a></sup> Gramme accidentally demonstrated it on the occasion of the <a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hippolyte_Fontaine#1873_:_l'expérience_décisive" title="fr:Hippolyte Fontaine">1873 Vienna World's Fair</a>, when he connected two such DC devices up to 2 km from each other, using one of them as a generator and the other as motor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamme2_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-gamme2-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>The drum rotor was introduced by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_von_Hefner-Alteneck" title="Friedrich von Hefner-Alteneck">Friedrich von Hefner-Alteneck</a> of Siemens &amp; Halske to replace Pacinotti's ring armature in 1872, thus improving the machine efficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dcmachine2_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-dcmachine2-6">[6]</a></sup> The laminated rotor was introduced by Siemens &amp; Halske the following year, achieving reduced iron losses and increased induced voltages. In 1880, <a href="/wiki/Jonas_Wenstr%C3%B6m" title="Jonas Wenström">Jonas Wenström</a> provided the rotor with slots for housing the winding, further increasing the efficiency.
</p><p>In 1886, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frank_Julian_Sprague" title="Frank Julian Sprague">Frank Julian Sprague</a> invented the first practical DC motor, a non-sparking device that maintained relatively constant speed under variable loads. Other Sprague electric inventions about this time greatly improved grid electric distribution (prior work done while employed by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">Thomas Edison</a>), allowed power from electric motors to be returned to the electric grid, provided for electric distribution to trolleys via overhead wires and the trolley pole, and provided control systems for electric operations. This allowed Sprague to use electric motors to invent the first electric trolley system in 1887–88 in <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond, Virginia</a>, the electric elevator and control system in 1892, and the electric subway with independently powered centrally-controlled cars. The latter were first installed in 1892 in Chicago by the <a href="/wiki/South_Side_Elevated_Railroad" title="South Side Elevated Railroad">South Side Elevated Railroad</a>, where it became popularly known as the "<a href="/wiki/Chicago_%22L%22" title='Chicago "L"'>L</a>". Sprague's motor and related inventions led to an explosion of interest and use in electric motors for industry. The development of electric motors of acceptable efficiency was delayed for several decades by failure to recognize the extreme importance of an air gap between the rotor and stator. Efficient designs have a comparatively small air gap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ganot_(1881)2_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ganot_(1881)2-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[a]</a></sup> The St. Louis motor, long used in classrooms to illustrate motor principles, is inefficient for the same reason, as well as appearing nothing like a modern motor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Electric motors revolutionized industry. Industrial processes were no longer limited by power transmission using line shafts, belts, compressed air or hydraulic pressure. Instead, every machine could be equipped with its own power source, providing easy control at the point of use, and improving power transmission efficiency. Electric motors applied in agriculture eliminated human and animal muscle power from such tasks as handling grain or pumping water. Household uses (like in washing machines, dishwashers, fans, air conditioners and refrigerators (replacing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_box" title="Ice box">ice boxes</a>)) of electric motors reduced heavy labor in the home and made higher standards of convenience, comfort and safety possible. Today, electric motors consume more than half of the electric energy produced in the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="AC_motors">AC motors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: AC motors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/AC_motor" title="AC motor">AC motor</a></div>
<p>In 1824, French physicist <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Arago" title="François Arago">François Arago</a> formulated the existence of <a href="/wiki/Rotating_magnetic_field" title="Rotating magnetic field">rotating magnetic fields</a>, termed <a href="/wiki/Arago%27s_rotations" title="Arago's rotations">Arago's rotations</a>, which, by manually turning switches on and off, Walter Baily demonstrated in 1879 as in effect the first primitive <a href="/wiki/Induction_motor" title="Induction motor">induction motor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Babbage_(1825)2_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Babbage_(1825)2-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_(1895)2_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson_(1895)2-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailey_(1879)2_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey_(1879)2-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vuckovic2_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vuckovic2-34">[33]</a></sup> In the 1880s many inventors were trying to develop workable AC motors<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jonnes_(2004)2_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jonnes_(2004)2-35">[34]</a></sup> because AC's advantages in long-distance high-voltage transmission were offset by the inability to operate motors on AC.
</p><p>The first alternating-current commutatorless induction motor was invented by <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Ferraris" title="Galileo Ferraris">Galileo Ferraris</a> in 1885. Ferraris was able to improve his first design by producing more advanced setups in 1886.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acmachine2_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-acmachine2-36">[35]</a></sup> In 1888, the <i>Royal Academy of Science of Turin</i> published Ferraris's research detailing the foundations of motor operation, while concluding at that time that "the apparatus based on that principle could not be of any commercial importance as motor."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vuckovic2_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vuckovic2-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferraris_(1888)2_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ferraris_(1888)2-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TFI_(now)2_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-TFI_(now)2-38">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Possible industrial development was envisioned by <a href="/wiki/Nikola_Tesla" title="Nikola Tesla">Nikola Tesla</a>, who invented independently his induction motor in 1887 and obtained a patent in May 1888. In the same year, Tesla presented his paper <i>A New System of Alternate Current Motors and Transformers</i> to the <a href="/wiki/American_Institute_of_Electrical_Engineers" title="American Institute of Electrical Engineers">AIEE</a> that described three patented two-phase four-stator-pole motor types: one with a four-pole rotor forming a non-self-starting <a href="/wiki/Reluctance_motor" title="Reluctance motor">reluctance motor</a>, another with a wound rotor forming a self-starting <a href="/wiki/Induction_motor" title="Induction motor">induction motor</a>, and the third a true <a href="/wiki/Synchronous_motor" title="Synchronous motor">synchronous motor</a> with separately excited DC supply to rotor winding. One of the patents Tesla filed in 1887, however, also described a shorted-winding-rotor induction motor. <a href="/wiki/George_Westinghouse" title="George Westinghouse">George Westinghouse</a>, who had already acquired rights from Ferraris (US$1,000), promptly bought Tesla's patents (US$60,000 plus US$2.50 per sold hp, paid until 1897),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acmachine2_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-acmachine2-36">[35]</a></sup> employed Tesla to develop his motors, and assigned <a href="/wiki/Charles_F._Scott_(engineer)" title="Charles F. Scott (engineer)">C.F. Scott</a> to help Tesla; however, Tesla left for other pursuits in 1889.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alger_(1976)2_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alger_(1976)2-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klooster_(2009)2_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klooster_(2009)2-40">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day_(1996)2_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Day_(1996)2-41">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Froehlich_(1992)2_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Froehlich_(1992)2-42">[41]</a></sup> The constant speed AC induction motor was found not to be suitable for street cars,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jonnes_(2004)2_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jonnes_(2004)2-35">[34]</a></sup> but Westinghouse engineers successfully adapted it to power a mining operation in Telluride, Colorado in 1891.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maddox_(2003)2_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maddox_(2003)2-43">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes_(1993)2_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes_(1993)2-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> Westinghouse achieved its first practical induction motor in 1892 and developed a line of polyphase 60 hertz induction motors in 1893, but these early Westinghouse motors were two-phase motors with wound rotors. <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_G._Lamme" title="Benjamin G. Lamme">B.G. Lamme</a> later developed a rotating bar winding rotor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alger_(1976)2_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alger_(1976)2-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Steadfast in his promotion of three-phase development, <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Dolivo-Dobrovolsky" title="Mikhail Dolivo-Dobrovolsky">Mikhail Dolivo-Dobrovolsky</a> invented the three-phase induction motor in 1889, of both types cage-rotor and wound rotor with a starting rheostat, and the three-limb <a href="/wiki/Transformer" title="Transformer">transformer</a> in 1890. After an agreement between AEG and <a href="/wiki/Maschinenfabrik_Oerlikon" title="Maschinenfabrik Oerlikon">Maschinenfabrik Oerlikon</a>, Doliwo-Dobrowolski and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Eugene_Lancelot_Brown" title="Charles Eugene Lancelot Brown">Charles Eugene Lancelot Brown</a> developed larger models, namely a 20-hp squirrel cage and a 100-hp wound rotor with a starting rheostat. These were the first three-phase asynchronous motors suitable for practical operation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acmachine2_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-acmachine2-36">[35]</a></sup> Since 1889, similar developments of three-phase machinery were started Wenström. At the 1891 Frankfurt International Electrotechnical Exhibition, the first long distance three-phase system was successfully presented. It was rated 15 kV and extended over 175 km from the Lauffen waterfall on the Neckar river. The Lauffen power station included a 240 kW 86 V 40 Hz alternator and a step-up transformer while at the exhibition a step-down transformer fed a 100-hp three-phase induction motor that powered an artificial waterfall, representing the transfer of the original power source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acmachine2_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-acmachine2-36">[35]</a></sup> The three-phase induction is now used for the vast majority of commercial applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEEE_German_Ch._(2012)2_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEEE_German_Ch._(2012)2-47">[46]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Dolivo-Dobrovolsky" title="Mikhail Dolivo-Dobrovolsky">Mikhail Dolivo-Dobrovolsky</a> claimed that Tesla's motor was not practical because of two-phase pulsations, which prompted him to persist in his three-phase work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dolivo-Dobrowolsky_(1891)2_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dolivo-Dobrowolsky_(1891)2-48">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/General_Electric_Company" title="General Electric Company">General Electric Company</a> began developing three-phase induction motors in 1891.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alger_(1976)2_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-Alger_(1976)2-39">[38]</a></sup> By 1896, General Electric and Westinghouse signed a cross-licensing agreement for the bar-winding-rotor design, later called the <a href="/wiki/Squirrel-cage_rotor" title="Squirrel-cage rotor">squirrel-cage rotor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alger_(1976)2_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-Alger_(1976)2-39">[38]</a></sup> Induction motor improvements flowing from these inventions and innovations were such that a 100-<a href="/wiki/Horsepower" title="Horsepower">horsepower</a> induction motor currently has the same mounting dimensions as a 7.5-horsepower motor in 1897.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alger_(1976)2_39-4"><a href="#cite_note-Alger_(1976)2-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Twenty-first_century">Twenty-first century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Twenty-first century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2022, electric motor sales were estimated to be 800 million units, increasing by 10% annually. Electric motors consume ~50% of the world's electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Components">Components</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Components">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stator_and_rotor_by_Zureks.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1709" data-file-width="2412" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Stator_and_rotor_by_Zureks.JPG/220px-Stator_and_rotor_by_Zureks.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Stator_and_rotor_by_Zureks.JPG/330px-Stator_and_rotor_by_Zureks.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Stator_and_rotor_by_Zureks.JPG/440px-Stator_and_rotor_by_Zureks.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stator_and_rotor_by_Zureks.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Electric motor rotor (left) and stator (right)</div></div></div>
<p>The two mechanical parts of an electric motor are the rotor, which moves, and the stator, which does not. It also includes two electrical parts, a set of magnets and an armature, one of which is attached to the rotor and the other to the stator, together forming a <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_circuit" title="Magnetic circuit">magnetic circuit</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scarpino2_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scarpino2-50">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Field_winding" title="Field winding">Field magnets</a> - The magnets create a magnetic field that passes through the armature. These can be <a href="/wiki/Electromagnet" title="Electromagnet">electromagnets</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permanent_magnet" title="Permanent magnet">permanent magnets</a>. The field magnet is usually on the stator and the armature on the rotor, but in some types of motor these are reversed.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bearings">Bearings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Bearings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The rotor is supported by <a href="/wiki/Bearing_(mechanical)" title="Bearing (mechanical)">bearings</a>, which allow the rotor to turn on its axis. The bearings are in turn supported by the motor housing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-51">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rotor">Rotor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Rotor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rotor_(electric)" title="Rotor (electric)">Rotor (electric)</a></div>
<p>The rotor is the moving part that delivers the mechanical power. The rotor typically holds conductors that carry currents, which the magnetic field of the stator exerts force on to turn the shaft. Alternatively, some rotors carry permanent magnets, and the stator holds the conductors. Permanent magnets offer high efficiency over a larger operating speed and power range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Induction2_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Induction2-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>An air gap between the stator and rotor allows it to turn. The width of the gap has a significant effect on the motor's electrical characteristics. It is generally made as small as possible, as a large gap weakens performance. It is the main source of the low <a href="/wiki/Power_factor" title="Power factor">power factor</a> at which motors operate. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetizing_current" title="Magnetizing current">magnetizing current</a> increases and the power factor decreases with the air gap, so narrow gaps are better. Conversely, gaps that are too small may pose mechanical problems in addition to noise and losses.
</p><p>The motor shaft extends through the bearings to the outside of the motor, where the load is applied. Because the forces of the load are exerted beyond the outermost bearing, the load is said to be overhung.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-51">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stator">Stator</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Stator">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Stator" title="Stator">Stator</a></div>
<p>The stator surrounds the rotor, and usually holds field magnets, which are either <a href="/wiki/Electromagnet" title="Electromagnet">electromagnets</a> consisting of wire windings around a ferromagnetic iron core or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permanent_magnet" title="Permanent magnet">permanent magnets</a>. These create a <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> that passes through the rotor armature, exerting force on the windings. The stator core is made up of many thin metal sheets that are insulated from each other, called laminations. Laminations are used to reduce energy losses that would result if a solid core were used. Resin-packed motors, used in washing machines and air conditioners, use the damping properties of <a href="/wiki/Resin" title="Resin">resin</a> (plastic) to reduce noise and vibration. These motors <a href="/wiki/Potting_(electronics)" title="Potting (electronics)">encapsulate the stator in plastic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Salient-pole_rotor.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="322" data-file-width="617" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Salient-pole_rotor.png/220px-Salient-pole_rotor.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Salient-pole_rotor.png/330px-Salient-pole_rotor.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Salient-pole_rotor.png/440px-Salient-pole_rotor.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Salient-pole_rotor.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Salient-pole rotor</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Armature">Armature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Armature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_coil" title="Electromagnetic coil">Electromagnetic coil</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Armature_(electrical)" title="Armature (electrical)">armature</a> consists of wire windings on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferromagnetic" title="Ferromagnetic">ferromagnetic</a> core. Electric current passing through the wire causes the <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> from the field magnet to exert a force (<a href="/wiki/Lorentz_force" title="Lorentz force">Lorentz force</a>) on it, turning the rotor, which delivers the mechanical output. Windings are wires that are laid in <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_coil" title="Electromagnetic coil">coils</a>, usually wrapped around a laminated, soft, iron, <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_core" title="Magnetic core">ferromagnetic core</a> so as to form magnetic poles when energized with current.
</p><p>Electric machines come in salient- and nonsalient-pole configurations. In a salient-pole motor the ferromagnetic cores on the rotor and stator have projections called poles facing each other, with a wire winding around each pole below the pole face, which become north or south poles of the <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> when current flows through the wire. In a nonsalient-pole (or distributed field or round-rotor) motor, the ferromagnetic core is a smooth cylinder, with the windings distributed evenly in slots about the circumference. Supplying alternating current in the windings creates poles in the core that rotate continuously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mortensen_(1949)2_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mortensen_(1949)2-54">[53]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Shaded-pole_motor" title="Shaded-pole motor">shaded-pole motor</a> has a winding around part of the pole that delays the phase of the magnetic field for that pole.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commutator">Commutator</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Commutator">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Commutator_(electric)" title="Commutator (electric)">Commutator (electric)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Universal_motor_commutator.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="479" data-file-width="816" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Universal_motor_commutator.jpg/220px-Universal_motor_commutator.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Universal_motor_commutator.jpg/330px-Universal_motor_commutator.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Universal_motor_commutator.jpg/440px-Universal_motor_commutator.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Universal_motor_commutator.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Commutator in a <a href="/wiki/Universal_motor" title="Universal motor">universal motor</a> from a vacuum cleaner. Parts: <i>(A)</i> commutator, <i>(B)</i> brush</div></div></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Commutator_(electric)" title="Commutator (electric)">commutator</a> is a rotary <a href="/wiki/Switch" title="Switch">electrical switch</a> that supplies current to the rotor. It periodically reverses the flow of current in the rotor windings as the shaft rotates. It consists of a cylinder composed of multiple metal contact segments on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armature_(electrical_engineering)" title="Armature (electrical engineering)">armature</a>. Two or more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_contacts" title="Electrical contacts">electrical contacts</a> called "<a href="/wiki/Brush_(electric)" title="Brush (electric)">brushes</a>" made of a soft conductive material like <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> press against the commutator. The brushes make sliding contact with successive commutator segments as it rotates, supplying current to the rotor. The windings on the rotor are connected to the commutator segments. The commutator periodically reverses the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Current_(electricity)" title="Current (electricity)">current</a> direction in the rotor windings with each half turn (180°), so the torque applied to the rotor is always in the same direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHameyer200162_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHameyer200162-55">[54]</a></sup> Without this current reversal, the direction of torque on each rotor winding would reverse with each half turn, so the rotor would stop. Commutators are inefficient and commutated motors have been mostly replaced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brushless_DC_motor" title="Brushless DC motor">brushless direct current motors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Permanent_magnet_motor" title="Permanent magnet motor">permanent magnet motors</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Induction_motor" title="Induction motor">induction motors</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Motor_supply_and_control">Motor supply and control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Motor supply and control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Motor_supply">Motor supply</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Motor supply">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A DC motor is usually supplied through a split ring commutator as described above. 
</p><p>AC motors' commutation can be achieved using either a slip ring commutator or external commutation. It can be fixed-speed or variable-speed control type, and can be synchronous or asynchronous. <a href="/wiki/Universal_motor" title="Universal motor">Universal motors</a> can run on either AC or DC.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Motor_control">Motor control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Motor control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>DC motors can be operated at variable speeds by adjusting the voltage applied to the terminals or by using <a href="/wiki/Pulse-width_modulation" title="Pulse-width modulation">pulse-width modulation</a> (PWM).
</p><p>AC motors operated at a fixed speed are generally powered directly from the grid or through <a href="/wiki/Motor_soft_starter" title="Motor soft starter">motor soft starters</a>.
</p><p>AC motors operated at variable speeds are powered with various <a href="/wiki/Power_inverter" title="Power inverter">power inverter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Variable-frequency_drive" title="Variable-frequency drive">variable-frequency drive</a> or electronic commutator technologies.
</p><p>The term electronic commutator is usually associated with self-commutated <a href="/wiki/Brushless_DC_electric_motor" title="Brushless DC electric motor">brushless DC motor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Switched_reluctance_motor" title="Switched reluctance motor">switched reluctance motor</a> applications.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Electric motors operate on one of three physical principles: <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">magnetism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electrostatic_motor" title="Electrostatic motor">electrostatics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Piezoelectric_motor" title="Piezoelectric motor">piezoelectricity</a>.
</p><p>In magnetic motors, magnetic fields are formed in both the rotor and the stator. The product between these two fields gives rise to a force, and thus a torque on the motor shaft. One, or both, of these fields must change with the rotation of the rotor. This is done by switching the poles on and off at the right time, or varying the strength of the pole.
</p><p>The main types are DC motors and AC motors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> with the latter replacing the former.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="There are some applications in which the reverse is true, however, this editor found no concrete numbers one way or the other. (December 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>AC electric motors are either asynchronous or synchronous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Once started, a synchronous motor requires synchrony with the moving magnetic field's speed for all normal torque conditions.
</p><p>In synchronous machines, the magnetic field must be provided by means other than induction, such as from separately excited windings or permanent magnets.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Fractional-horsepower_motor" title="Fractional-horsepower motor">fractional-horsepower motor</a> either has a rating below about 1 horsepower (0.746 kW), or is manufactured with a standard-frame size smaller than a standard 1 HP motor. Many household and industrial motors are in the fractional-horsepower class.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Type of motor commutation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDOEVSPGuide2_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-USDOEVSPGuide2-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBose2006328,_397,_481_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBose2006328,_397,_481-60">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_(UA)2_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-King_(UA)2-61">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHameyer2001_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHameyer2001-62">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stolting_(2008)2_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stolting_(2008)2-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ionel_(2010)2_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ionel_(2010)2-64">[63]</a></sup>

<th>Electronic commutator<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ionel_(2010)2_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ionel_(2010)2-64">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[b]</a></sup>
</th>

<p>Notes:
</p>
<ul><li><span id="CharacterizatioNote1">Rotation is independent of the frequency of the AC voltage.</span></li>
<li><span id="CharacterizatioNote2">Rotation is equal to synchronous speed (motor-stator-field speed).</span></li>
<li><span id="CharacterizatioNote3">In SCIM, fixed-speed operation rotation is equal to synchronous speed, less slip speed.</span></li>
<li><span id="CharacterizatioNote4">In non-slip <a href="/wiki/Energy_recovery" title="Energy recovery">energy-recovery</a> systems, WRIM is usually used for motor-starting but can be used to vary load speed.</span></li>
<li><span id="CharacterizatioNote5">Variable-speed operation.</span></li>
<li><span id="CharacterizatioNote6">Whereas induction- and synchronous-motor drives are typically with either six-step or sinusoidal-waveform output, BLDC-motor drives are usually with trapezoidal-current waveform; the behavior of both sinusoidal and trapezoidal PM machines is, however, identical in terms of their fundamental aspects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krishnan_(2008)2_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krishnan_(2008)2-70">[67]</a></sup></span></li>
<li><span id="CharacterizatioNote7">In variable-speed operation, WRIM is used in slip-energy recovery and double-fed induction-machine applications.</span></li>
<li><span id="CharacterizatioNote8">A cage winding is a shorted-circuited squirrel-cage rotor, a wound winding is connected externally through slip rings.</span></li>
<li>Mostly single-phase with some three-phase.</li></ul>

</p>

<li>SCIM – <a href="/wiki/Squirrel-cage_rotor" title="Squirrel-cage rotor">Squirrel-cage</a> <a href="/wiki/Induction_motor" title="Induction motor">induction motor</a></li>
