<li>WRSM – <a href="/wiki/Synchronous_motor" title="Synchronous motor">Wound-rotor synchronous motor</a></li>
<li>LRA – Locked-rotor amps: The current you can expect under starting conditions when you apply full voltage. It occurs instantly during start-up.</li>
<li>RLA – Rated-load amps: The maximum current a motor should draw under any operating conditions. Often mistakenly called running-load amps, which leads people to believe, incorrectly, that the motor should always pull these amps.</li>
<li>FLA – Full-load amps: Changed in 1976 to "RLA – rated-load amps".</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-commutated_motor">Self-commutated motor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Self-commutated motor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brushed_DC_motor">Brushed DC motor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Brushed DC motor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/DC_motor" title="DC motor">DC motor</a></div>
<p>Most DC motors are small permanent magnet (PM) types. They contain a <a href="/wiki/Brush_(electric)" title="Brush (electric)">brushed</a> internal mechanical commutation to reverse motor windings' current in synchronism with rotation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weiβmantel_(2008)2_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weiβmantel_(2008)2-71">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrically_excited_DC_motor">Electrically excited DC motor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Electrically excited DC motor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Brushed_DC_electric_motor" title="Brushed DC electric motor">Brushed DC electric motor</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Electric_motor_cycle_2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Electric_motor_cycle_2.png/220px-Electric_motor_cycle_2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Electric_motor_cycle_2.png/330px-Electric_motor_cycle_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Electric_motor_cycle_2.png/440px-Electric_motor_cycle_2.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Electric_motor_cycle_2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Workings of a brushed electric motor with a two-pole rotor and PM stator. ("N" and "S" designate polarities on the inside faces of the magnets; the outside faces have opposite polarities.)</div></div></div>
<p>A commutated DC motor has a set of rotating windings wound on an <a href="/wiki/Armature_(electrical)" title="Armature (electrical)">armature</a> mounted on a rotating shaft. The shaft also carries the commutator. Thus, every brushed DC motor has AC flowing through its windings. Current flows through one or more pairs of brushes that touch the commutator; the brushes connect an external source of electric power to the rotating armature.
</p><p>The rotating armature consists of one or more wire coils wound around a laminated, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soft_magnetic_material" title="Soft magnetic material">magnetically "soft"</a> ferromagnetic core. Current from the brushes flows through the commutator and one winding of the armature, making it a temporary magnet (an <a href="/wiki/Electromagnet" title="Electromagnet">electromagnet</a>). The magnetic field produced interacts with a stationary magnetic field produced by either PMs or another winding (a field coil), as part of the motor frame. The force between the two magnetic fields rotates the shaft. The commutator switches power to the coils as the rotor turns, keeping the poles from ever fully aligning with the magnetic poles of the stator field, so that the rotor keeps turning as long as power is applied.
</p><p>Many of the limitations of the classic commutator DC motor are due to the need for brushes to maintain contact with the commutator, creating friction. The brushes create sparks while crossing the insulating gaps between commutator sections. Depending on the commutator design, the brushes may create <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Short_circuits" title="Short circuits">short circuits</a> between adjacent sections—and hence coil ends. Furthermore, the rotor coils' <a href="/wiki/Inductance" title="Inductance">inductance</a> causes the voltage across each to rise when its circuit opens, increasing the sparking. This sparking limits the maximum speed of the machine, as too-rapid sparking will overheat, erode, or even melt the commutator. The current density per unit area of the brushes, in combination with their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resistivity" title="Resistivity">resistivity</a>, limits the motor's output. Crossing the gaps also generates <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_noise" title="Electrical noise">electrical noise</a>; sparking generates <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_frequency_interference" title="Radio frequency interference">RFI</a>. Brushes eventually wear out and require replacement, and the commutator itself is subject to wear and maintenance or replacement. The commutator assembly on a large motor is a costly element, requiring precision assembly of many parts. On small motors, the commutator is usually permanently integrated into the rotor, so replacing it usually requires replacing the rotor.
</p><p>While most commutators are cylindrical, some are flat, segmented discs mounted on an insulator.
</p><p>Large brushes create a large contact area, which maximizes motor output, while small brushes have low mass to maximize the speed at which the motor can run without excessive sparking. (Small brushes are desirable for their lower cost.) Stiffer brush springs can be used to make brushes of a given mass work at a higher speed, despite greater friction losses (lower efficiency) and accelerated brush and commutator wear. Therefore, DC motor brush design entails a trade-off between output power, speed, and efficiency/wear.
</p><p>DC machines are defined as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorf_(1997)2_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorf_(1997)2-72">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Armature circuit – A winding that carries the load, either stationary or rotating.</li>
<li>Field circuit – A set of windings that produces a magnetic field.</li>
<li>Commutation: A mechanical technique in which rectification can be achieved, or from which DC can be derived.</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Serie_Shunt_Coumpound.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="370" data-file-width="1030" decoding="async" height="79" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Serie_Shunt_Coumpound.svg/220px-Serie_Shunt_Coumpound.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Serie_Shunt_Coumpound.svg/330px-Serie_Shunt_Coumpound.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Serie_Shunt_Coumpound.svg/440px-Serie_Shunt_Coumpound.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Serie_Shunt_Coumpound.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A: shunt B: series C: compound f = field coil</div></div></div>
<p>The five types of brushed DC motor are:
</p>
<ul><li>Shunt-wound</li>
<li>Series-wound</li>
<li>Compound (two configurations):
<ul><li>Cumulative compound</li>
<li>Differentially compounded</li></ul></li>
<li>Permanent magnet (not shown)</li>
<li>Separately excited (not shown).</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Permanent_magnet">Permanent magnet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Permanent magnet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permanent-magnet_electric_motor" title="Permanent-magnet electric motor">Permanent-magnet electric motor</a></div>
<p>A permanent magnet (PM) motor does not have a field winding on the stator frame, relying instead on PMs to provide the magnetic field. Compensating windings in series with the armature may be used on large motors to improve commutation under load. This field is fixed and cannot be adjusted for speed control. PM fields (stators) are convenient in miniature motors to eliminate the power consumption of the field winding. Most larger DC motors are of the "dynamo" type, which have stator windings. Historically, PMs could not be made to retain high flux if they were disassembled; field windings were more practical to obtain the needed flux. However, large PMs are costly, as well as dangerous and difficult to assemble; this favors wound fields for large machines.
</p><p>To minimize overall weight and size, miniature PM motors may use high energy magnets made with <a href="/wiki/Neodymium" title="Neodymium">neodymium</a>; most are neodymium-iron-boron alloy. With their higher flux density, electric machines with high-energy PMs are at least competitive with all optimally designed <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor#Singly_fed_electric_motor" title="Electric motor">singly-fed</a> synchronous and induction electric machines. Miniature motors resemble the structure in the illustration, except that they have at least three rotor poles (to ensure starting, regardless of rotor position) and their outer housing is a steel tube that magnetically links the exteriors of the curved field magnets.
</p>
<h3><span id="Electronic_commutator_.28EC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Electronic_commutator_(EC)">Electronic commutator (EC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Electronic commutator (EC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Brushless_DC">Brushless DC</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Brushless DC">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Brushless_DC_electric_motor" title="Brushless DC electric motor">Brushless DC electric motor</a></div>
<p>Some of the problems of the brushed DC motor are eliminated in the BLDC design. In this motor, the mechanical "rotating switch" or commutator is replaced by an external electronic switch synchronised to the rotor's position. BLDC motors are typically 85%+ efficient, reaching up to 96.5%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nozawa_(2009)2_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nozawa_(2009)2-73">[70]</a></sup> while brushed DC motors are typically 75–80% efficient.
</p><p>The BLDC motor's characteristic trapezoidal <a href="/wiki/Counter-electromotive_force" title="Counter-electromotive force">counter-electromotive force</a> (CEMF) waveform is derived partly from the stator windings being evenly distributed, and partly from the placement of the rotor's permanent magnets. Also known as electronically commutated DC or inside-out DC motors, the stator windings of trapezoidal BLDC motors can be single-phase, two-phase or three-phase and use <a href="/wiki/Hall_effect_sensor" title="Hall effect sensor">Hall effect sensors</a> mounted on their windings for rotor position sensing and low cost <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Closed-loop_controller" title="Closed-loop controller">closed-loop commutator control</a>.
</p><p>BLDC motors are commonly used where precise speed control is necessary, as in computer disk drives or video cassette recorders. The spindles within CD, CD-ROM (etc.) drives, and mechanisms within office products, such as fans, laser printers and photocopiers. They have several advantages over conventional motors:
</p>
<ul><li>They are more efficient than AC fans using shaded-pole motors, running much cooler than the AC equivalents. This cool operation leads to much-improved life of the fan's bearings.</li>
<li>Without a commutator, the life of a BLDC motor can be significantly longer compared to a brushed DC motor with a commutator. Commutation tends to cause electrical and RF noise; without a commutator or brushes, a BLDC motor may be used in electrically sensitive devices like audio equipment or computers.</li>
<li>The same <a href="/wiki/Hall_effect" title="Hall effect">Hall effect</a> sensors that provide the commutation can provide a convenient <a href="/wiki/Tachometer" title="Tachometer">tachometer</a> signal for closed-loop control (servo-controlled) applications. In fans, the tachometer signal can be used to derive a "fan OK" signal as well as provide running speed feedback.</li>
<li>The motor can be synchronized to an internal or external clock, providing precise speed control.</li>
<li>BLDC motors do not spark, making them better suited to environments with volatile chemicals and fuels. Sparking also generates ozone, which can accumulate in poorly ventilated buildings.</li>
<li>BLDC motors are usually used in small equipment such as computers and are generally used in fans to remove heat.</li>
<li>They make little noise, which is an advantage in equipment that is affected by vibrations.</li></ul>
<p>Modern BLDC motors range in power from a fraction of a watt to many kilowatts. Larger BLDC motors rated up to about 100 kW are used in electric vehicles. They also find use in electric <a href="/wiki/Model_aircraft" title="Model aircraft">model aircraft</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Switched_reluctance_motor">Switched reluctance motor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Switched reluctance motor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Switched_reluctance_motor" title="Switched reluctance motor">Switched reluctance motor</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Switched_reluctance_motor_6-4.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Switched_reluctance_motor_6-4.svg/220px-Switched_reluctance_motor_6-4.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Switched_reluctance_motor_6-4.svg/330px-Switched_reluctance_motor_6-4.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Switched_reluctance_motor_6-4.svg/440px-Switched_reluctance_motor_6-4.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Switched_reluctance_motor_6-4.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>6/4 pole switched reluctance motor</div></div></div>
<p>The switched reluctance motor (SRM) has no brushes or permanent magnets, and the rotor has no electric currents. Torque comes from a slight misalignment of poles on the rotor with poles on the stator. The rotor aligns itself with the magnetic field of the stator, while the stator field windings are sequentially energized to rotate the stator field.
</p><p>The magnetic flux created by the field windings follows the path of least magnetic sending the flux through rotor poles that are closest to the energized poles of the stator, thereby magnetizing those poles of the rotor and creating torque. As the rotor turns, different windings are energized, keeping the rotor turning.
</p><p>SRMs are used in some appliances<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bush_(2009)2_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bush_(2009)2-74">[71]</a></sup> and vehicles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Universal_AC.2FDC_motor"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Universal_AC/DC_motor">Universal AC/DC motor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Universal AC/DC motor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Universal_motor" title="Universal motor">Universal motor</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Universalmotor_3.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Universalmotor_3.JPG/220px-Universalmotor_3.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Universalmotor_3.JPG/330px-Universalmotor_3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Universalmotor_3.JPG/440px-Universalmotor_3.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Universalmotor_3.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Modern low-cost universal motor, from a vacuum cleaner. Field windings are dark copper-colored, toward the back, on both sides. The rotor's laminated core is gray metallic, with dark slots for winding the coils. The commutator (partly hidden) has become dark from use; it is toward the front. The large brown molded-plastic piece in the foreground supports the brush guides and brushes (both sides), as well as the front motor bearing.</div></div></div>
<p>A commutated, electrically excited, series or parallel wound motor is referred to as a universal motor because it can be designed to operate on either AC or DC power. A universal motor can operate well on AC because the current in both the field and the armature coils (and hence the resultant magnetic fields)  synchronously reverse polarity, and hence the resulting mechanical force occurs in a constant direction of rotation.
</p><p>Operating at normal <a href="/wiki/Utility_frequency" title="Utility frequency">power line frequencies</a>, universal motors are often used in sub-kilowatt applications. Universal motors formed the basis of the traditional railway traction motor in <a href="/wiki/Railway_electrification_system#Low-frequency_alternating_current" title="Railway electrification system">electric railways</a>. In this application, using AC power on a motor designed to run on DC would experience efficiency losses due to <a href="/wiki/Eddy_current" title="Eddy current">eddy current</a> heating of their magnetic components, particularly the motor field pole-pieces that, for DC, would have used solid (un-laminated) iron. They are now rarely used.
</p><p>An advantage is that AC power may be used on motors that specifically have high starting torque and compact design if high running speeds are used. By contrast, maintenance is higher and lifetimes are shortened. Such motors are used in devices that are not heavily used, and have high starting-torque demands. Multiple taps on the field coil provide (imprecise) stepped speed control. Household blenders that advertise many speeds typically combine a field coil with several taps and a diode that can be inserted in series with the motor (causing the motor to run on half-wave rectified AC). Universal motors also lend themselves to <a href="/wiki/TRIAC#Application" title="TRIAC">electronic speed control</a> and, as such, are a choice for devices such as domestic washing machines. The motor can agitate the drum (both forwards and in reverse) by switching the field winding with respect to the armature.
</p><p>Whereas SCIMs cannot turn a shaft faster than allowed by the power line frequency, universal motors can run at much higher speeds. This makes them useful for appliances such as blenders, vacuum cleaners, and hair dryers where high speed and light weight are desirable. They are also commonly used in portable power tools, such as drills, sanders, circular and jig saws, where the motor's characteristics work well. Many vacuum cleaner and weed trimmer motors exceed <span class="nowrap">10,000 rpm</span>, while miniature grinders may exceed <span class="nowrap">30,000 rpm</span>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Externally_commutated_AC_machine">Externally commutated AC machine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Externally commutated AC machine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/AC_motor" title="AC motor">AC motor</a></div>
<p>AC induction and synchronous motors are optimized for operation on single-phase or polyphase sinusoidal or quasi-sinusoidal waveform power such as supplied for fixed-speed applications by the AC power grid or for variable-speed application from <a href="/wiki/Variable-frequency_drive" title="Variable-frequency drive">variable-frequency drive</a> (VFD) controllers. 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Induction_motor">Induction motor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Induction motor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Induction_motor" title="Induction motor">Induction motor</a></div><p>An induction motor is an asynchronous AC motor where power is transferred to the rotor by electromagnetic induction, much like transformer action. An induction motor resembles a rotating transformer, because the stator (stationary part) is essentially the primary side of the transformer and the rotor (rotating part) is the secondary side. Polyphase induction motors are widely used in industry.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elliott-Motor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1275" data-file-width="1700" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Elliott-Motor.jpg/220px-Elliott-Motor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Elliott-Motor.jpg/330px-Elliott-Motor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Elliott-Motor.jpg/440px-Elliott-Motor.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elliott-Motor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Large 4,500 hp AC induction motor</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cage_and_wound_rotor">Cage and wound rotor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Cage and wound rotor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Induction motors may be divided into <a href="/wiki/Squirrel-cage_rotor" title="Squirrel-cage rotor">Squirrel Cage Induction Motors</a> (SCIM) and <a href="/wiki/Wound_rotor_motor" title="Wound rotor motor">Wound Rotor Induction Motors</a> (WRIM). SCIMs have a heavy winding made up of solid bars, usually aluminum or copper, electrically connected by rings at the ends of the rotor. The bars and rings as a whole are much like an animal's rotating exercise cage.
</p><p>Currents induced into this winding provide the rotor magnetic field. The shape of the rotor bars determines the speed-torque characteristics. At low speeds, the current induced in the squirrel cage is nearly at line frequency and tends to stay in the outer parts of the cage. As the motor accelerates, the slip frequency becomes lower, and more current reaches the interior. By shaping the bars to change the resistance of the winding portions in the interior and outer parts of the cage, a variable resistance is effectively inserted in the rotor circuit. However, most such motors employ uniform bars.
</p><p>In a WRIM, the rotor winding is made of many turns of insulated wire and is connected to <a href="/wiki/Slip_ring" title="Slip ring">slip rings</a> on the motor shaft. An external resistor or other control device can be connected in the rotor circuit. Resistors allow control of the motor speed, although dissipating significant power. A converter can be fed from the rotor circuit and return the slip-frequency power that would otherwise be wasted into the power system through an inverter or separate motor-generator.
</p><p>WRIMs are used primarily to start a high inertia load or a load that requires high starting torque across the full speed range. By correctly selecting the resistors used in the secondary resistance or slip ring starter, the motor is able to produce maximum torque at a relatively low supply current from zero speed to full speed. 
</p><p>Motor speed can be changed because the motor's torque curve is effectively modified by the amount of resistance connected to the rotor circuit. Increasing resistance lowers the speed of maximum torque. If the resistance is increased beyond the point where the maximum torque occurs at zero speed, the torque is further reduced.
</p><p>When used with a load that has a torque curve that increases with speed, the motor operates at the speed where the torque developed by the motor is equal to the load torque. Reducing the load causes the motor to speed up, while increasing the load causes the motor to slow down until the load and motor torque are again equal. Operated in this manner, the slip losses are dissipated in the secondary resistors and can be significant. The speed regulation and net efficiency is poor.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Torque_motor">Torque motor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Torque motor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Torque_motor" title="Torque motor">Torque motor</a></div>
<p>A torque motor can operate indefinitely while stalled, that is, with the rotor blocked from turning, without incurring damage. In this mode of operation, the motor applies a steady torque to the load.
</p><p>A common application is the supply- and take-up reel motors in a tape drive. In this application, driven by a low voltage, the characteristics of these motors apply a steady light tension to the tape whether or not the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capstan_(tape_recorder)" title="Capstan (tape recorder)">capstan</a> is feeding tape past the tape heads. Driven from a higher voltage (delivering a higher torque), torque motors can achieve fast-forward and rewind operation without requiring additional mechanics such as gears or clutches. In the computer gaming world, torque motors are used in force feedback steering wheels.
</p><p>Another common application is to control the throttle of an internal combustion engine with an electronic governor. The motor works against a return spring to move the throttle in accord with the governor output. The latter monitors engine speed by counting electrical pulses from the ignition system or from a magnetic pickup and depending on the speed, makes small adjustments to the amount of current. If the engine slows down relative to the desired speed, the current increases, producing more torque, pulling against the return spring and opening the throttle. Should the engine run too fast, the governor reduces the current, allowing the return spring to pull back and reduce the throttle.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Synchronous_motor">Synchronous motor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Synchronous motor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Synchronous_motor" title="Synchronous motor">Synchronous motor</a></div>
<p>A synchronous electric motor is an AC motor. It includes a rotor spinning with coils passing magnets at the same frequency as the AC and produces a magnetic field to drive it. It has zero slip under typical operating conditions. By contrast induction motors must slip to produce torque. One type of synchronous motor is like an induction motor except that the rotor is excited by a DC field. Slip rings and brushes conduct current to the rotor. The rotor poles connect to each other and move at the same speed. Another type, for low load torque, has flats ground onto a conventional squirrel-cage rotor to create discrete poles. Yet another, as made by Hammond for its pre-World War II clocks, and in older <a href="/wiki/Hammond_organ" title="Hammond organ">Hammond organs</a>, has no rotor windings and discrete poles. It is not self-starting. The clock requires manual starting by a small knob on the back, while the older Hammond organs had an auxiliary starting motor connected by a spring-loaded manually operated switch.
</p><p>Hysteresis synchronous motors typically are (essentially) two-phase motors with a phase-shifting capacitor for one phase. They start like induction motors, but when slip rate decreases sufficiently, the rotor (a smooth cylinder) becomes temporarily magnetized. Its distributed poles make it act like a permanent magnet synchronous motor. The rotor material, like that of a common nail, stays magnetized, but can be demagnetized with little difficulty. Once running, the rotor poles stay in place; they do not drift.
</p><p>Low-power synchronous timing motors (such as those for traditional electric clocks) may have multi-pole permanent magnet external cup rotors, and use shading coils to provide starting torque. <i><a href="/wiki/Telechron" title="Telechron">Telechron</a></i> clock motors have shaded poles for starting torque, and a two-spoke ring rotor that performs like a discrete two-pole rotor.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Doubly-fed_electric_machine">Doubly-fed electric machine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Doubly-fed electric machine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Doubly-fed_electric_machine" title="Doubly-fed electric machine">Doubly-fed electric machine</a></div>
<p>Doubly fed electric motors have two independent multiphase winding sets, which contribute active (i.e., working) power to the energy conversion process, with at least one of the winding sets electronically controlled for variable speed operation. Two independent multiphase winding sets (i.e., dual armature) are the maximum provided in a single package without topology duplication. Doubly-fed electric motors have an effective constant torque speed range that is twice synchronous speed for a given frequency of excitation. This is twice the constant torque speed range as <a href="/wiki/Singly-fed_electric_machine" title="Singly-fed electric machine">singly-fed electric machines</a>, which have only one active winding set.
</p><p>A doubly-fed motor allows for a smaller electronic converter but the cost of the rotor winding and slip rings may offset the saving in the power electronics components. Difficulties affect controlling speed near synchronous speed limit applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lander_(1993)2_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lander_(1993)2-76">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Special_magnetic_motors">Special magnetic motors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Special magnetic motors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rotary">Rotary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Rotary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ironless_or_coreless_rotor_motor">Ironless or coreless rotor motor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Ironless or coreless rotor motor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Miniature_Coreless_DC_Motor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1552" data-file-width="1965" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Miniature_Coreless_DC_Motor.jpg/220px-Miniature_Coreless_DC_Motor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Miniature_Coreless_DC_Motor.jpg/330px-Miniature_Coreless_DC_Motor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Miniature_Coreless_DC_Motor.jpg/440px-Miniature_Coreless_DC_Motor.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Miniature_Coreless_DC_Motor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A miniature coreless motor</div></div></div>
<p>The coreless or ironless DC motor is a specialized permanent magnet DC motor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weiβmantel_(2008)2_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Weiβmantel_(2008)2-71">[68]</a></sup> Optimized for rapid <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">acceleration</a>, the rotor is constructed without an iron core. The rotor can take the form of a winding-filled cylinder, or a self-supporting structure comprising only wire and bonding material. The rotor can fit inside the stator magnets; a magnetically soft stationary cylinder inside the rotor provides a return path for the stator magnetic flux. A second arrangement has the rotor winding basket surrounding the stator magnets. In that design, the rotor fits inside a magnetically soft cylinder that can serve as the motor housing, and provides a return path for the flux.
</p><p>Because the rotor is much lower mass than a conventional rotor, it can accelerate much more rapidly, often achieving a mechanical <a href="/wiki/Time_constant" title="Time constant">time constant</a> under one millisecond. This is especially true if the windings use aluminum rather than (heavier) copper. The rotor has no metal mass to act as a heat sink; even small motors must be cooled. Overheating can be an issue for these designs.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Vibrating_alert" title="Vibrating alert">vibrating alert</a> of cellular phones can be generated by cylindrical permanent-magnet motors, or disc-shaped types that have a thin multipolar disc field magnet, and an intentionally unbalanced molded-plastic rotor structure with two bonded coreless coils. Metal brushes and a flat commutator switch power to the rotor coils.
</p><p>Related limited-travel actuators have no core and a bonded coil placed between the poles of high-flux thin permanent magnets. These are the fast head positioners for rigid-disk ("hard disk") drives. Although the contemporary design differs considerably from that of loudspeakers, it is still loosely (and incorrectly) referred to as a "voice coil" structure, because some earlier rigid-disk-drive heads moved in straight lines, and had a drive structure much like that of a loudspeaker.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pancake_or_axial_rotor_motor">Pancake or axial rotor motor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Pancake or axial rotor motor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Axial_flux_motor" title="Axial flux motor">Axial flux motor</a></div>
<p>The printed armature or pancake motor has windings shaped as a disc running between arrays of high-flux magnets. The magnets are arranged in a circle facing the rotor spaced to form an axial air gap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krishnan_(1987)2_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krishnan_(1987)2-77">[74]</a></sup> This design is commonly known as the pancake motor because of its flat profile.
</p><p>The armature (originally formed on a printed circuit board) is made from punched copper sheets that are laminated together using advanced composites to form a thin, rigid disc. The armature does not have a separate ring commutator. The brushes move directly on the armature surface making the whole design compact.
</p><p>An alternative design is to use wound copper wire laid flat with a central conventional commutator, in a flower and petal shape. The windings are typically stabilized with electrical epoxy potting systems. These are filled epoxies that have moderate, mixed viscosity and a long gel time. They are highlighted by low shrinkage and low exotherm, and are typically UL 1446 recognized as a potting compound insulated with 180 °C (356 °F), Class H rating.
</p><p>The unique advantage of ironless DC motors is the absence of <a href="/wiki/Cogging_torque" title="Cogging torque">cogging</a> (torque variations caused by changing attraction between the iron and the magnets). Parasitic eddy currents cannot form in the rotor as it is totally ironless, although iron rotors are laminated. This can greatly improve efficiency, but variable-speed controllers must use a higher switching rate (&gt;40 kHz) or DC because of decreased <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_induction" title="Electromagnetic induction">electromagnetic induction</a>.
</p><p>These motors were invented to drive the capstan(s) of magnetic tape drives, where minimal time to reach operating speed and minimal stopping distance were critical. Pancake motors are widely used in high-performance servo-controlled systems, robotic systems, industrial automation and medical devices. Due to the variety of constructions now available, the technology is used in applications from high temperature military to low cost pump and basic servos.
</p><p>Another approach (Magnax) is to use a single stator sandwiched between two rotors. One such design has produced peak power of 15 kW/kg, sustained power around 7.5 kW/kg. This yokeless axial flux motor offers a shorter flux path, keeping the magnets further from the axis. The design allows zero winding overhang; 100 percent of the windings are active. This is enhanced with the use of rectangular-crosssection copper wire. The motors can be stacked to work in parallel. Instabilities are minimized by ensuring that the two rotor discs put equal and opposing forces onto the stator disc. The rotors are connected directly to one another via a shaft ring, cancelling out the magnetic forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-78">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Magnax motors range in size from 0.15–5.4 metres (5.9 in – 17 ft 8.6 in) in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-:02-78">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Servo_motor">Servo motor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Servo motor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Servo_motor" title="Servo motor">Servo motor</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Servomotor" title="Servomotor">servo motor</a> is a motor that is used within a position-control or speed-control feedback system. Servomotors are used in applications such as machine tools, pen plotters, and other process systems. Motors intended for use in a servomechanism must have predictable characteristics for speed, torque, and power. The speed/torque curve is important and is high ratio for a servo motor. Dynamic response characteristics such as winding inductance and rotor inertia are important; these factors limit performance. Large, powerful, but slow-responding servo loops may use conventional AC or DC motors and drive systems with position or speed feedback. As dynamic response requirements increase, more specialized motor designs such as coreless motors are used. AC motors' superior power density and acceleration characteristics tends to favor permanent magnet synchronous, BLDC, induction, and SRM drive approaches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krishnan_(1987)2_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Krishnan_(1987)2-77">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>A servo system differs from some stepper motor applications in that position feedback is continuous while the motor is running. A stepper system inherently operates open-loop—relying on the motor not to "miss steps" for short term accuracy—with any feedback such as a "home" switch or position encoder external to the motor system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patrick_(1997)2_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patrick_(1997)2-79">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stepper_motor">Stepper motor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Stepper motor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Stepper_motor" title="Stepper motor">Stepper motor</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stepper_motor.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="808" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Stepper_motor.svg/220px-Stepper_motor.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Stepper_motor.svg/330px-Stepper_motor.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Stepper_motor.svg/440px-Stepper_motor.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stepper_motor.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A stepper motor with a soft iron rotor, with active windings shown. In 'A' the active windings tend to hold the rotor in position. In 'B' a different set of windings are carrying a current, which generates torque and rotation.</div></div></div>
<p>Stepper motors are typically used to provide precise rotations. An internal rotor containing permanent magnets or a magnetically soft rotor with salient poles is controlled by a set of electronically switched external magnets. A stepper motor may also be thought of as a cross between a DC electric motor and a rotary solenoid. As each coil is energized in turn, the rotor aligns itself with the magnetic field produced by the energized field winding. Unlike a synchronous motor, the stepper motor may not rotate continuously; instead, it moves in steps—starting and then stopping—advancing from one position to the next as field windings are energized and de-energized in sequence. Depending on the sequence, the rotor may turn forwards or backwards, and it may change direction, stop, speed up or slow down at any time.
</p><p>Simple stepper motor drivers entirely energize or entirely de-energize the field windings, leading the rotor to "cog" to a limited number of positions. <a href="/wiki/Stepper_motor#Microstepping" title="Stepper motor">Microstepping</a> drivers can proportionally control the power to the field windings, allowing the rotors to position between cog points and rotate smoothly. Computer-controlled stepper motors are one of the most versatile positioning systems, particularly as part of a digital <a href="/wiki/Servomechanism" title="Servomechanism">servo-controlled</a> system.
</p><p>Stepper motors can be rotated to a specific angle in discrete steps with ease, and hence stepper motors are used for read/write head positioning in early <a href="/wiki/Hard_disk_drive" title="Hard disk drive">disk drives</a>, where the precision and speed they offered could correctly position the read/write head. As drive density increased, precision and speed limitations made them obsolete for hard drives—the precision limitation made them unusable, and the speed limitation made them uncompetitive—thus newer hard disk drives use voice coil-based head actuator systems. (The term "voice coil" in this connection is historic; it refers to the structure in a cone-type <a href="/wiki/Loudspeaker" title="Loudspeaker">loudspeaker</a>.)
</p><p>Stepper motors are often used in computer printers, optical scanners, and digital photocopiers to move the active element, the print head carriage (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inkjet_printers" title="Inkjet printers">inkjet printers</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Platen" title="Platen">platen</a> or feed rollers.
</p><p>So-called quartz analog wristwatches contain the smallest commonplace stepping motors; they have one coil, draw little power, and have a permanent magnet rotor. The same kind of motor drives battery-powered quartz clocks. Some of these watches, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chronographs" title="Chronographs">chronographs</a>, contain more than one stepper motor.
</p><p>Closely related in design to three-phase AC synchronous motors, stepper motors and SRMs are classified as variable reluctance motor type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBose2006569–70,_891_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBose2006569–70,_891-80">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Linear_motor">Linear motor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Linear motor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Linear_motor" title="Linear motor">Linear motor</a></div>
<p>A linear motor is essentially any electric motor that has been "unrolled" so that, instead of producing <a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">torque</a> (rotation), it produces a straight-line force along its length.
</p><p>Linear motors are most commonly <a href="/wiki/Linear_induction_motor" title="Linear induction motor">induction motors</a> or stepper motors. Linear motors are commonly found in roller-coasters where the rapid motion of the motorless railcar is controlled by the rail. They are also used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maglev_train" title="Maglev train">maglev trains</a>, where the train "flies" over the ground. On a smaller scale, the 1978 era HP 7225A pen plotter used two linear stepper motors to move the pen along the X and Y axes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Electromagnetism">Electromagnetism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Electromagnetism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">March 2013</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Force_and_torque">Force and torque</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Force and torque">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An electric motor converts electrical energy to mechanical energy through the force between two opposed magnetic fields. At least one of the two magnetic fields must be created by an electromagnet through the magnetic field caused by an electrical current.
</p><p>The force between a current <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle I}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>I</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle I}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="I" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/535ea7fc4134a31cbe2251d9d3511374bc41be9f" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.172ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> in a conductor of length <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \ell }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>ℓ<!-- ℓ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \ell }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\ell " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f066e981e530bacc07efc6a10fa82deee985929e" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.97ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> perpendicular to a magnetic field <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {B} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">B</mi>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbf {B} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbf {B} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cafb0ef39b0f5ffa23c170aa7f7b4e718327c4d1" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.901ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> may be calculated using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorentz_force_law#Force_on_a_current-carrying_wire" title="Lorentz force law">Lorentz force law</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =I\ell \times \mathbf {B} \,\!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">F</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>I</mi>
<mi>ℓ<!-- ℓ --></mi>
<mo>×<!-- × --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">B</mi>
</mrow>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mspace width="negativethinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =I\ell \times \mathbf {B} \,\!}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =I\ell \times \mathbf {B} \,\!}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a915ace5dc31bcd23e2bd37dd225ee43e278ba1e" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; margin-right: -0.387ex; width:12.051ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>Note: X denotes vector <a href="/wiki/Cross_product" title="Cross product">cross product</a>. 
</p><p>The most general approaches to calculating the forces in motors use tensor notation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirtley_(2005)2_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kirtley_(2005)2-92">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power">Power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \omega _{rpm}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>ω<!-- ω --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>r</mi>
<mi>p</mi>
<mi>m</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \omega _{rpm}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \omega _{rpm}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ce7806cfefe675066708ff69bdb0f810e7fa8e0e" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:4.689ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span> is shaft speed in <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_per_minute" title="Revolutions per minute">rpm</a> and T is torque, a motor's mechanical power output P<sub>em</sub> is given by,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>in <a href="/wiki/Imperial_units" title="Imperial units">Imperial units</a> with T expressed in foot-pounds,
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle P_{em}={\frac {\omega _{rpm}T}{5252}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>P</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>e</mi>
<mi>m</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<msub>
<mi>ω<!-- ω --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>r</mi>
<mi>p</mi>
<mi>m</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mi>T</mi>
</mrow>
<mn>5252</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P_{em}={\frac {\omega _{rpm}T}{5252}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle P_{em}={\frac {\omega _{rpm}T}{5252}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/107951a56f3f534cecdafabd3013a08285d9b5c6" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:14.194ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span> (horsepower), and,</dd></dl>
<p>in <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">SI units</a> with shaft <a href="/wiki/Angular_frequency" title="Angular frequency">angular speed</a>, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \omega }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>ω<!-- ω --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \omega }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\omega " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/48eff443f9de7a985bb94ca3bde20813ea737be8" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.446ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span>, expressed in radians per second, and T expressed in newton-meters,
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle P_{em}=\omega T}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>P</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>e</mi>
<mi>m</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>ω<!-- ω --></mi>
<mi>T</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P_{em}=\omega T}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle P_{em}=\omega T}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5d8c90e215a0944a0eb716f1348ba24c4cf54a71" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:10.114ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> (watts).</dd></dl>
<p>For a linear motor, with force F expressed in newtons and velocity v expressed in meters per second,
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle P_{em}=F{v}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Back_EMF">Back EMF</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Back EMF">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Electromotive_force" title="Electromotive force">Electromotive force</a></div>
<p>The movement of armature windings of a direct-current or universal motor through a magnetic field, induce a voltage in them. This voltage tends to oppose the motor supply voltage and so is called "<a href="/wiki/Electromotive_force" title="Electromotive force">back electromotive force (EMF)</a>". The voltage is proportional to the running speed of the motor. The back EMF of the motor, plus the voltage drop across the winding internal resistance and brushes, must equal the voltage at the brushes. This provides the fundamental mechanism of speed regulation in a DC motor. If the mechanical load increases, the motor slows down; a lower back EMF results, and more current is drawn from the supply. This increased current provides the additional torque to balance the load.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dwight_(1949)2_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dwight_(1949)2-94">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>In AC machines, it is sometimes useful to consider a back EMF source within the machine; this is of particular concern for close speed regulation of induction motors on VFDs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dwight_(1949)2_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dwight_(1949)2-94">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Losses">Losses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Losses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Losses_in_electrical_systems" title="Losses in electrical systems">Motor losses</a> are mainly due to <a href="/wiki/Joule_heating" title="Joule heating">resistive losses</a> in windings, core losses and mechanical losses in bearings, and aerodynamic losses, particularly where cooling fans are present, also occur.
</p><p>Losses also occur in commutation, mechanical commutators spark; electronic commutators and also dissipate heat.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Efficiency">Efficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Efficiency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

It is possible to derive analytically the point of maximum efficiency. It is typically at less than 1/2 the <a href="/wiki/Stall_torque" title="Stall torque">stall torque</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Various national regulatory authorities have enacted legislation to encourage the manufacture and use of higher-efficiency motors. Electric motors have efficiencies ranging from at least 15% for shaded pole motors, up to 98% for permanent magnet motors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[93]</a></sup> with efficiency also dependent on load. Peak efficiency is usually at 75% of the rated load. So (as an example) a 10 HP motor is most efficient when driving a load that requires 7.5 HP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[94]</a></sup> Efficiency also depends on motor size; larger motors tend to be more efficient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[95]</a></sup> Some motors can not operate continually for more than a specified period of time (e.g. for more than an hour per run)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[96]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>From this, he showed that the most efficient motors are likely to have relatively large magnetic poles. However, the equation only directly relates to non PM motors.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Performance_parameters">Performance parameters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Performance parameters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Torque">Torque</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Torque">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Generalize plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="" data-file-height="40" data-file-width="40" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>describes only one highly specialized aspect of its associated subject</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by adding more general information. The <a href="/wiki/Talk:Electric_motor" title="Talk:Electric motor">talk page</a> may contain suggestions.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">March 2012</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table><p>Electromagnetic motors derive torque from the vector product of the interacting fields. Calculating torque requires knowledge of the fields in the air gap. Once these have been established, the torque is the integral of all the force vectors multiplied by the vector's radius. The current flowing in the winding produces the fields. For a motor using a magnetic material the field is not proportional to the current.
</p><p>A figure relating the current to the torque can inform motor selection. The maximum torque for a motor depends on the maximum current, absent thermal considerations.
</p><p>When optimally designed within a given core saturation constraint and for a given active current (i.e., torque current), voltage, pole-pair number, excitation frequency (i.e., synchronous speed), and air-gap flux density, all categories of electric motors/generators exhibit virtually the same maximum continuous shaft torque (i.e., operating torque) within a given air-gap area with winding slots and back-iron depth, which determines the physical size of electromagnetic core. Some applications require bursts of torque beyond the maximum, such as bursts to accelerate an electric vehicle from standstill. Always limited by <a href="/wiki/Saturation_(magnetic)" title="Saturation (magnetic)">magnetic core saturation</a> or safe <a href="/wiki/Operating_temperature" title="Operating temperature">operating temperature</a> rise and voltage, the capacity for torque bursts beyond the maximum differs significantly across motor/generator types.
</p><p>Electric machines without a transformer circuit topology, such as that of WRSMs or PMSMs, cannot provide torque bursts without saturating the magnetic core. At that point, additional current cannot increase torque. Furthermore, the permanent magnet assembly of PMSMs can be irreparably damaged.
</p><p>Electric machines with a transformer circuit topology, such as induction machines, induction doubly-fed electric machines, and induction or synchronous wound-rotor doubly-fed (WRDF) machines, permit torque bursts because the EMF-induced active current on either side of the transformer oppose each other and thus contribute nothing to the transformer coupled magnetic core flux density, avoiding core saturation.
</p><p>Electric machines that rely on induction or asynchronous principles short-circuit one port of the transformer circuit and as a result, the reactive impedance of the transformer circuit becomes dominant as slip increases, which limits the magnitude of active (i.e., real) current. Torque bursts two to three times higher than the maximum design torque are realizable.
</p><p>The brushless wound-rotor synchronous doubly-fed (BWRSDF) machine is the only electric machine with a truly dual ported transformer circuit topology (i.e., both ports independently excited with no short-circuited port).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klatt_(2012)2_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klatt_(2012)2-102">[99]</a></sup> The dual ported transformer circuit topology is known to be unstable and requires a multiphase slip-ring-brush assembly to propagate limited power to the rotor winding set. If a precision means were available to instantaneously control torque angle and slip for synchronous operation during operation while simultaneously providing brushless power to the rotor winding set, the active current of the BWRSDF machine would be independent of the reactive impedance of the transformer circuit and bursts of torque significantly higher than the maximum operating torque and far beyond the practical capability of any other type of electric machine would be realizable. Torque bursts greater than eight times operating torque have been calculated.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Continuous_torque_density">Continuous torque density</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Continuous torque density">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The continuous torque density of conventional electric machines is determined by the size of the air-gap area and the back-iron depth, which are determined by the power rating of the armature winding set, the speed of the machine, and the achievable air-gap flux density before core saturation. Despite the high coercivity of neodymium or <a href="/wiki/Samarium" title="Samarium">samarium</a>-cobalt permanent magnets, continuous torque density is virtually the same amongst electric machines with optimally designed armature winding sets. Continuous torque density relates to method of cooling and permissible operation period before destruction by overheating of windings or permanent magnet damage.
</p><p>Other sources state that various e-machine topologies have differing torque density. One source shows the following:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Electric machine type
</th>
<th>Specific torque density (Nm/kg)
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>SPM – brushless ac, 180° current conduction
</td>
<td>1.0
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>SPM – brushless ac, 120° current conduction
</td>
<td>0.9–1.15
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>IM, asynchronous machine
</td>
<td>0.7–1.0
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>IPM, interior permanent magnet machine
</td>
<td>0.6–0.8
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>VRM, doubly salient reluctance machine
</td>
<td>0.7–1.0
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>where—specific torque density is normalized to 1.0 for the surface permanent magnet (SPM)—brushless ac, 180° current conduction.
</p><p>Torque density is approximately four times greater for liquid cooled motors, compared to those which are air cooled.
</p><p>A source comparing direct current, induction motors (IM), PMSM and SRM showed:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
