</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Another source notes that PMSM up to 1 MW have considerably higher torque density than induction machines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Continuous_power_density">Continuous power density</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Continuous power density">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The continuous power density is determined by the product of the continuous torque density and the constant torque speed range. Electric motors can achieve densities of up to 20KW/KG, meaning 20 Kilowatts of output power per kilogram.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Acoustic_noise_and_vibrations">Acoustic noise and vibrations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Acoustic noise and vibrations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Acoustic noise and vibrations are usually classified in three sources:
</p>
<ul><li>mechanical sources (e.g. due to <a href="/wiki/Bearing_(mechanical)" title="Bearing (mechanical)">bearings</a>)</li>
<li>aerodynamic sources (e.g. due to shaft-mounted <a href="/wiki/Fan_(machine)" title="Fan (machine)">fans</a>)</li>
<li>magnetic sources (e.g. due to magnetic forces such as <a href="/wiki/Maxwell_stress_tensor" title="Maxwell stress tensor">Maxwell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetostriction" title="Magnetostriction">magnetostriction</a> forces acting on stator and rotor structures)</li></ul>
<p>The latter source, which can be responsible for the "whining noise" of electric motors, is called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetically_induced_acoustic_noise_and_vibration" title="Electromagnetically induced acoustic noise and vibration">electromagnetically induced acoustic noise</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Standards">Standards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Standards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The following are major design, manufacturing, and testing standards covering electric motors:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/American_Petroleum_Institute" title="American Petroleum Institute">American Petroleum Institute</a>: API 541 Form-Wound Squirrel Cage Induction Motors – 375 kW (500 Horsepower) and Larger</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/American_Petroleum_Institute" title="American Petroleum Institute">American Petroleum Institute</a>: API 546 Brushless Synchronous Machines – 500 kVA and Larger</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/American_Petroleum_Institute" title="American Petroleum Institute">American Petroleum Institute</a>: API 547 General-purpose Form-Wound Squirrel Cage Induction Motors – 250 Hp and Larger</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Electrical_and_Electronics_Engineers" title="Institute of Electrical and Electronics Engineers">Institute of Electrical and Electronics Engineers</a>: IEEE Std 112 Standard Test Procedure for Polyphase Induction Motors and Generators</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Electrical_and_Electronics_Engineers" title="Institute of Electrical and Electronics Engineers">Institute of Electrical and Electronics Engineers</a>: IEEE Std 115 Guide for Test Procedures for Synchronous Machines</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Electrical_and_Electronics_Engineers" title="Institute of Electrical and Electronics Engineers">Institute of Electrical and Electronics Engineers</a>: IEEE Std 841 Standard for Petroleum and Chemical Industry – Premium Efficiency Severe Duty Totally Enclosed Fan-Cooled (TEFC) Squirrel Cage Induction Motors – Up to and Including 370 kW (500 Hp)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Electrotechnical_Commission" title="International Electrotechnical Commission">International Electrotechnical Commission</a>: IEC 60034 Rotating Electrical Machines</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Electrotechnical_Commission" title="International Electrotechnical Commission">International Electrotechnical Commission</a>: IEC 60072 Dimensions and output series for rotating electrical machines</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/National_Electrical_Manufacturers_Association" title="National Electrical Manufacturers Association">National Electrical Manufacturers Association</a>: <a class="external text" href="http://www.nema.org/Standards/Pages/Information-Guide-for-General-Purpose-Industrial-AC-Small-and-Medium-Squirrel-Cage-Induction-Motor-Standards.aspx" rel="nofollow">MG-1 Motors and Generators</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Underwriters_Laboratories" title="Underwriters Laboratories">Underwriters Laboratories</a>: UL 1004 – Standard for Electric Motors</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_Standard" title="Indian Standard">Indian Standard</a>: IS:12615-2018 – Line Operated Three Phase a.c. Motors (IE CODE) “Efficiency Classes and Performance Specification” (Third Revision)</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-magnetic_motors">Non-magnetic motors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Non-magnetic motors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Electrostatic_motor" title="Electrostatic motor">Electrostatic motor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Piezoelectric_motor" title="Piezoelectric motor">Piezoelectric motor</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrically_powered_spacecraft_propulsion" title="Electrically powered spacecraft propulsion">Electrically powered spacecraft propulsion</a></div>
<p>An electrostatic motor is based on the attraction and repulsion of electric charge. Usually, electrostatic motors are the dual of conventional coil-based motors. They typically require a high-voltage power supply, although small motors employ lower voltages. Conventional electric motors instead employ magnetic attraction and repulsion, and require high current at low voltages. In the 1750s, the first electrostatic motors were developed by Benjamin Franklin and Andrew Gordon. Electrostatic motors find frequent use in micro-electro-mechanical systems (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MEMS" title="MEMS">MEMS</a>) where their drive voltages are below 100 volts, and where moving, charged plates are far easier to fabricate than coils and iron cores. The molecular machinery that runs living cells is often based on linear and rotary electrostatic motors.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>A piezoelectric motor or piezo motor is a type of electric motor based upon the change in shape of a <a href="/wiki/Piezoelectricity" title="Piezoelectricity">piezoelectric material</a> when an <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric field</a> is applied. Piezoelectric motors make use of the converse piezoelectric effect whereby the material produces acoustic or <a href="/wiki/Ultrasonic_motor" title="Ultrasonic motor">ultrasonic</a> vibrations to produce linear or rotary motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[104]</a></sup> In one mechanism, the elongation in a single plane is used to make a series of stretches and position holds, similar to the way a caterpillar moves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>An electrically powered spacecraft propulsion system uses electric motor technology to propel spacecraft in outer space. Most systems are based on electrically accelerating propellant to high speed, while some systems are based on <a href="/wiki/Electrodynamic_tether" title="Electrodynamic tether">electrodynamic tethers</a> principles of propulsion to the <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[106]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_motor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ganot provides a superb illustration of one such early electric motor designed by Froment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ganot_(1881)_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ganot_(1881)-27">[27]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-66">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The term 'electronic commutator motor' (ECM) is identified with the heating, ventilation and air-conditioning (HVAC) industry, the distinction between BLDC and BLAC being in this context seen as a function of degree of ECM drive complexity with BLDC drives typically being with simple single-phase <a href="/wiki/Scalar_(mathematics)" title="Scalar (mathematics)">scalar-controlled</a> voltage-regulated trapezoidal current waveform output involving surface PM motor construction and BLAC drives tending towards more complex three-phase <a href="/wiki/Vector_control_(motor)" title="Vector control (motor)">vector-controlled</a> current-regulated sinusoidal waveform involving interior PM motor construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ionel_(2010)_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ionel_(2010)-65">[64]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The universal and repulsion motors are part of a class of motors known as AC commutator motors, which also includes the following now largely obsolete motor types: Single-phase – straight and compensated series motors, railway motor; three-phase – various repulsion motor types, brush-shifting series motor, brush-shifting polyphase shunt or Schrage motor, Fynn-Weichsel motor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alger_(1949b)_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alger_(1949b)-68">[66]</a></sup></span>
</li>
